      <p>
       The
       <b>
        tilde
       </b>
       (
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">
          /
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">
            ˈ
           </span>
           <span title="'t' in 'tie'">
            t
           </span>
           <span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">
            ɪ
           </span>
           <span title="'l' in 'lie'">
            l
           </span>
           <span title="'d' in 'dye'">
            d
           </span>
           <span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">
            ə
           </span>
          </span>
          /
         </a>
        </span>
       </span>
       ;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ah_1-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ah-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <b>
        ˜
       </b>
       or
       <b>
        ~
       </b>
       )
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       is a
       <a href="/wiki/Grapheme" title="Grapheme">
        grapheme
       </a>
       with several uses. The name of the character came into English from
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       and from
       <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">
        Portuguese
       </a>
       , which in turn came from the
       <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">
        Latin
       </a>
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/titulus" title="wikt:titulus">
         titulus
        </a>
       </i>
       , meaning "title" or "superscription".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ah_1-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ah-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The reason for the name was that it was originally written over a letter as a
       <a href="/wiki/Scribal_abbreviation" title="Scribal abbreviation">
        scribal abbreviation
       </a>
       , as a "mark of suspension", shown as a straight line when used with capitals. Thus the commonly used words
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Anno_Domini" title="Anno Domini">
         Anno Domini
        </a>
       </i>
       were frequently abbreviated to
       <i>
        A
        <sup>
         o
        </sup>
        Dñi
       </i>
       , an elevated terminal with a suspension mark placed over the "n". Such a mark could denote the omission of one letter or several letters. This saved on the expense of the scribe's labour and the cost of vellum and ink. Medieval European charters written in Latin are largely made up of such abbreviated words with suspension marks and other abbreviations; only uncommon words were given in full. The tilde has since been applied to a number of other uses as a
       <a href="/wiki/Diacritic" title="Diacritic">
        diacritic
       </a>
       mark or a character in its own right. These are encoded in
       <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">
        Unicode
       </a>
       at
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="monospaced" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">
         U+0303
        </span>
       </span>
       <span style="font-size:125%">
        ◌̃
       </span>
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-size: smaller;">
        Combining Tilde
       </span>
       and
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="monospaced" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">
         U+007E
        </span>
       </span>
       <span style="font-size:125%">
        ~
       </span>
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-size: smaller;">
        Tilde
       </span>
       (as a spacing character), and there are
       <a href="#Similar_characters">
        additional similar characters
       </a>
       for different roles. In
       <a href="/wiki/Lexicography" title="Lexicography">
        lexicography
       </a>
       , the latter kind of tilde and the
       <b>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swung_dash" title="Swung dash">
         swung dash
        </a>
       </b>
       (⁓) are used in dictionaries to indicate the omission of the entry word.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Common_use">
        Common use
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tilde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Common use">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       This symbol (in English) informally
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wolfram_tilde_4-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-wolfram_tilde-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       means
       <a href="/wiki/Approximation" title="Approximation">
        "approximately"
       </a>
       , "about", or "around", such as "~30 minutes before", meaning "
       <i>
        approximately
       </i>
       30 minutes before".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bymath1_5-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-bymath1-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It can mean "similar to",
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-htmlhelp1_7-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-htmlhelp1-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       including "of the same
       <a href="/wiki/Order_of_magnitude" title="Order of magnitude">
        order of magnitude
       </a>
       as",
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wolfram_tilde_4-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-wolfram_tilde-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       such as: "
       <span class="texhtml">
        <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">
         x
        </span>
        ~
        <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">
         y
        </span>
       </span>
       " meaning that
       <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">
        x
       </span>
       and
       <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">
        y
       </span>
       are of the same
       <a href="/wiki/Order_of_magnitude" title="Order of magnitude">
        order of magnitude
       </a>
       . Another approximation symbol is the double-tilde
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%E2%89%88" title="≈">
        ≈
       </a>
       , meaning "approximately equal to",
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bymath1_5-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-bymath1-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-htmlhelp1_7-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-htmlhelp1-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the critical difference being the subjective level of accuracy: ≈ indicates a value which can be considered functionally equivalent for a calculation within an acceptable degree of error, whereas ~ is usually used to indicate a larger, possibly significant, degree of error
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="I've never heard this before and a quick google doesn't turn up any support (September 2017)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       . The tilde is also used to indicate "equal to" or "approximately equal to" by placing it over the "=" symbol, like so:
       <b>
        ≅
       </b>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tilde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation">
       <tr>
        <td class="mbox-image">
         <div style="width:52px">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/>
          </a>
         </div>
        </td>
        <td class="mbox-text">
         <div class="mbox-text-span">
          This section
          <b>
           does not
           <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">

           </a>
           any
           <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">

           </a>
          </b>
          .
          <span class="hide-when-compact">

      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Use_by_medieval_scribes">
        Use by medieval scribes
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tilde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Use by medieval scribes">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MollandDB3.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="284" data-file-width="577" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/7b/MollandDB3.jpg/300px-MollandDB3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/7b/MollandDB3.jpg/450px-MollandDB3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/7/7b/MollandDB3.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MollandDB3.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Text of Exeter
         <a href="/wiki/Domesday_Book" title="Domesday Book">
          Domesday Book
         </a>
         of 1086
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The text of the
       <a href="/wiki/Domesday_Book" title="Domesday Book">
        Domesday Book
       </a>
       of 1086, relating for example, to the
       <a href="/wiki/Manor_of_Molland" title="Manor of Molland">
        manor of Molland
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Devon" title="Devon">
        Devon
       </a>
       (see image left), is highly
       <a href="/wiki/Scribal_abbreviation" title="Scribal abbreviation">
        abbreviated
       </a>
       as indicated by numerous tildes. The text with abbreviations expanded is as follows:
      </p>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>
        <i>
         <span lang="la" xml:lang="la">
          Mollande tempore regis Edwardi geldabat pro iiii hidis et uno ferling. Terra est xl carucis. In dominio sunt iii carucae et x servi et xxx villani et xx bordarii cum xvi carucis. Ibi xii acrae prati et xv acrae silvae. Pastura iii leugae in longitudine et latitudine. Libras ad pensam. Huic manerio est adjuncta Blachepole. Elwardus tenebat tempore regis Edwardi pro manerio et geldabat pro dimidia hida. Terra est ii carucis. Ibi sunt v villani cum i servo. Valet xx solidos ad pensam et arsuram. Eidem manerio est injuste adjuncta Nimete et valet xv solidos. Ipsi manerio pertinet tercius denarius de Hundredis Nortmoltone et Badentone et Brantone et tercium animal pasturae morarum.
         </span>
        </i>
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Role_of_mechanical_typewriters">
        Role of mechanical typewriters
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tilde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Role of mechanical typewriters">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The incorporation of the tilde (~) into
       <a href="/wiki/ASCII" title="ASCII">
        ASCII
       </a>
       is a direct result of its appearance as a distinct character on mechanical
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Typewriters" title="Typewriters">
        typewriters
       </a>
       in the late nineteenth century. When all
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Character_set" title="Character set">
        character sets
       </a>
       were pieces of metal permanently installed, and number of characters much more limited than in
       <a href="/wiki/Typography" title="Typography">
        typography
       </a>
       , the question of which languages and markets required which characters was an important one. Any good typewriter store had a catalog of alternative keyboards that could be specified for machines ordered from the factory.
      </p>
      <p>
       At that time, the tilde was used only in Spanish and Portuguese typewriters (keyboards). In Modern Spanish, the tilde is used only with
       <i>
        n
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        N
       </i>
       . Both were conveniently assigned to a single mechanical typebar, which sacrificed a key that was felt to be less important, usually the ​
       <span class="frac nowrap">
        <sup>
         1
        </sup>
        ⁄
        <sub>
         2
        </sub>
       </span>
       —​
       <span class="frac nowrap">
        <sup>
         1
        </sup>
        ⁄
        <sub>
         4
        </sub>
       </span>
       key.
      </p>
      <p>
       Portuguese, however, uses not
       <i>
        ñ
       </i>
       but
       <i>
        nh
       </i>
       . It uses the tilde on the vowels
       <i>
        a
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        o
       </i>
       . So as not to sacrifice two of the tightly limited keys to ã Ã õ Õ, the decision was made to make the ~ a separate "dead" character in which the carriage holding the paper did not move.
       <a href="/wiki/Dead_key" title="Dead key">
        Dead keys
       </a>
       , which had a notch cut out to avoid hitting a mechanical linkage that triggered carriage movement, were used for characters that were intended to be combined (overstruck).
      </p>
      <p>
       On mechanical typewriters, Spanish keyboards (the first, or one of the first, non-English keyboards) had a dead key, which contained the
       <a href="/wiki/Acute_accent" title="Acute accent">
        acute accent
       </a>
       (´), used over any vowel, and the
       <a href="/wiki/Diaeresis_(diacritic)" title="Diaeresis (diacritic)">
        dieresis
       </a>
       (¨), used only over
       <i>
        u
       </i>
       . It was a simple matter to create a dead key for a Portuguese keyboard (created later than the Spanish one) to be overstruck with
       <i>
        a
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        o
       </i>
       and so the ~ was born as a typographical character, which did not exist previously as a
       <a href="/wiki/Sort_(typesetting)" title="Sort (typesetting)">
        type
       </a>
       or
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Hot-lead&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Hot-lead (page does not exist)">
        hot-lead
       </a>
       printing character. That was probably a product of the first and leading manufacturer of (mechanical) typewriters,
       <a href="/wiki/E._Remington_and_Sons" title="E. Remington and Sons">
        Remington
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Connection_to_Spanish">
        Connection to Spanish
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tilde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Connection to Spanish">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/%C3%91" title="Ñ">
        Ñ
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Logotipo_del_Instituto_Cervantes.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="256" data-file-width="256" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Logotipo_del_Instituto_Cervantes.svg/220px-Logotipo_del_Instituto_Cervantes.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Logotipo_del_Instituto_Cervantes.svg/330px-Logotipo_del_Instituto_Cervantes.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Logotipo_del_Instituto_Cervantes.svg/440px-Logotipo_del_Instituto_Cervantes.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Logotipo_del_Instituto_Cervantes.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Logo of the Instituto Cervantes
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CNN_en_Espa%C3%B1ol.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="552" data-file-width="787" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/CNN_en_Espa%C3%B1ol.svg/220px-CNN_en_Espa%C3%B1ol.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/CNN_en_Espa%C3%B1ol.svg/330px-CNN_en_Espa%C3%B1ol.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/CNN_en_Espa%C3%B1ol.svg/440px-CNN_en_Espa%C3%B1ol.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CNN_en_Espa%C3%B1ol.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Logo of CNN en Español
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       As indicated by the etymological origin of the word "tilde" in English, this symbol has been closely associated with the
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish language
       </a>
       . The connection stems from the use of the tilde above the letter "n" to form "ñ" in Spanish, a feature shared by only
       <a href="/wiki/Tilde#Palatal_n" title="Tilde">
        a few other languages
       </a>
       , all historically connected to Spanish. This peculiarity can help non-native speakers quickly identify a text as being written in Spanish with little chance of error. In addition, most native speakers, although
       <a href="/wiki/Names_given_to_the_Spanish_language" title="Names given to the Spanish language">
        not all
       </a>
       , use the word "espa
       <b>
        ñ
       </b>
       ol" to refer to their language. Particularly during the 1990s, Spanish-speaking intellectuals and news outlets demonstrated support for the language and the culture by defending this letter against
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Globalisation" title="Globalisation">
        globalisation
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Computer" title="Computer">
        computerisation
       </a>
       trends that threatened to remove it from keyboards and other standardised products and codes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Instituto_Cervantes" title="Instituto Cervantes">
        Instituto Cervantes
       </a>
       , founded by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_government" title="Spanish government">
        Spain's government
       </a>
       to promote the Spanish language internationally, chose as its logo a highly stylised Ñ with a large tilde. A 24-hour news channel
       <a href="/wiki/CNN" title="CNN">
        CNN
       </a>
       in the US later adopted a similar strategy on its existing logo for the launch of its
       <a href="/wiki/CNN_en_Espa%C3%B1ol" title="CNN en Español">
        Spanish-language version
       </a>
       . And similarly to the
       <a href="/wiki/National_Basketball_Association" title="National Basketball Association">
        National Basketball Association
       </a>
       (NBA), the
       <a href="/wiki/Spain_men%27s_national_basketball_team" title="Spain men's national basketball team">
        Spain men's national basketball team
       </a>
       is nicknamed
       <i>
        ÑBA
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Confusingly, in Spanish itself the word
       <i>
        tilde
       </i>
       is used more generally for diacritics, including the stress-marking acute accent.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The diacritic
       <i>
        ~
       </i>
       is more commonly called
       <i>
        la virgulilla
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        la tilde de la eñe
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Diacritical_use">
        Diacritical use
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tilde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Diacritical use">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       In some languages, the tilde is used as a
       <a href="/wiki/Diacritic" title="Diacritic">
        diacritical
       </a>
       mark (
       <b>
        ˜
       </b>
       ) placed over a
       <a href="/wiki/Letter_(alphabet)" title="Letter (alphabet)">
        letter
       </a>
       to indicate a change in pronunciation, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Nasalization" title="Nasalization">
        nasalization
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Pitch">
        Pitch
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tilde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Pitch">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       It was first used in the
       <a href="/wiki/Greek_diacritics" title="Greek diacritics">
        polytonic orthography
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">
        Ancient Greek
       </a>
       , as a variant of the
       <a href="/wiki/Circumflex" title="Circumflex">
        circumflex
       </a>
       , representing a rise in
       <a href="/wiki/Pitch_accent" title="Pitch accent">
        pitch
       </a>
       followed by a return to standard pitch.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Abbreviation">
        Abbreviation
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tilde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Abbreviation">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hic_Fabricatur_Naves.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="333" data-file-width="414" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Hic_Fabricatur_Naves.jpg/220px-Hic_Fabricatur_Naves.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Hic_Fabricatur_Naves.jpg/330px-Hic_Fabricatur_Naves.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a2/Hic_Fabricatur_Naves.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hic_Fabricatur_Naves.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Carta_marina" title="Carta marina">
           Carta marina
          </a>
         </i>
         showing Finnish economy, with the captions
         <i>
          Hic fabricantur naves
         </i>
         and
         <i>
          Hic fabricantur
          <a href="/wiki/Bombard_(weapon)" title="Bombard (weapon)">
           bombarde
          </a>
         </i>
         abbreviated
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Later, it was used to make
       <a href="/wiki/Scribal_abbreviation" title="Scribal abbreviation">
        abbreviations
       </a>
       in medieval
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">
        Latin
       </a>
       documents. When an ⟨n⟩ or ⟨m⟩ followed a vowel, it was often omitted, and a tilde (i.e., a small ⟨n⟩) was placed over the preceding vowel to indicate the missing letter; this is the origin of the use of tilde to indicate nasalization (compare
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Umlaut_(diacritic)#Umlaut" title="Umlaut (diacritic)">
        the development of the umlaut
       </a>
       as an abbreviation of ⟨e⟩.) The practice of using the tilde over a vowel to indicate omission of an ⟨n⟩ or ⟨m⟩ continued in printed books in
       <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">
        French
       </a>
       as a means of reducing text length until the 17th century. It was also used in
       <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">
        Portuguese
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The tilde was also used occasionally to make other abbreviations, such as over the letter ⟨q⟩ ("
       <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/q%CC%83" title="wikt:q̃">
        q̃
       </a>
       ") to signify the word
       <i>
        que
       </i>
       ("that").
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Nasalization">
        Nasalization
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tilde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Nasalization">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       It is also as a small ⟨n⟩ that the tilde originated when written above other letters, marking a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">
        Latin
       </a>
       ⟨n⟩ which had been
       <a href="/wiki/Elision" title="Elision">
        elided
       </a>
       in old Galician-Portuguese. In modern
       <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">
        Portuguese
       </a>
       it indicates
       <a href="/wiki/Nasalization" title="Nasalization">
        nasalization
       </a>
       of the base vowel:
       <i>
        mão
       </i>
       "hand", from Lat.
       <i>
        manu-
       </i>
       ;
       <i>
        razões
       </i>
       "reasons", from Lat.
       <i>
        rationes
       </i>
       . This usage has been adopted in the orthographies of several
       <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_languages_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous languages of the Americas">
        native languages of South America
       </a>
       , such as
       <a href="/wiki/Guarani_language" title="Guarani language">
        Guarani
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nheengatu_language" title="Nheengatu language">
        Nheengatu
       </a>
       , as well as in the
       <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">
        International Phonetic Alphabet
       </a>
       (IPA) and many other phonetic alphabets. For example,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ljɔ̃]
       </span>
       is the IPA transcription of the pronunciation of the French place-name
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Lyon" title="Lyon">
         Lyon
        </a>
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Breton_language" title="Breton language">
        Breton
       </a>
       , the symbol ⟨ñ⟩ after a vowel means that the letter ⟨n⟩ serves only to give the vowel a nasalised pronunciation, without being itself pronounced, as it normally is. For example, ⟨an⟩ gives the pronunciation
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ãn]
       </span>
       whereas ⟨añ⟩ gives
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ã]
       </span>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Palatal_n">
        Palatal n
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tilde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Palatal n">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/%C3%91" title="Ñ">
        Ñ
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The tilded ⟨n⟩ (⟨ñ⟩, ⟨Ñ⟩) developed from the digraph ⟨nn⟩ in Spanish. In this language, ⟨ñ⟩ is considered a separate letter called
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/%C3%91" title="Ñ">
         eñe
        </a>
       </i>
       (
       <small>
        IPA:
       </small>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Spanish" title="Help:IPA/Spanish">
         [ˈeɲe]
        </a>
       </span>
       ), rather than a letter-diacritic combination; it is placed in Spanish dictionaries between the letters ⟨n⟩ and ⟨o⟩. In Spanish the word
       <i>
        tilde
       </i>
       can refer to diacritics in general, e.g. the acute accent in
       <i>
        José
       </i>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and the diacritic in ⟨ñ⟩ can also be called "virgulilla".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Current languages in which the tilded ⟨n⟩ (⟨ñ⟩) is used for the
       <a href="/wiki/Palatal_nasal" title="Palatal nasal">
        palatal nasal
       </a>
       consonant
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ɲ/
       </span>
       include:
      </p>
      <div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 8em; -webkit-column-width: 8em; column-width: 8em;">
       <ul>
        <li>
         <a href="/wiki/Asturian_language" title="Asturian language">
          Asturian
         </a>
        </li>
        <li>
         <a href="/wiki/Basque_language" title="Basque language">
          Basque
         </a>
        </li>
        <li>
         <a href="/wiki/Chamorro_language" title="Chamorro language">
          Chamorro
         </a>
        </li>
        <li>
         <a href="/wiki/Filipino_language" title="Filipino language">
          Filipino
         </a>
        </li>
        <li>
         <a href="/wiki/Galician_language" title="Galician language">
          Galician
         </a>
        </li>
        <li>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guaran%C3%AD_language" title="Guaraní language">
          Guaraní
         </a>
        </li>
        <li>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/I%C3%B1upiaq" title="Iñupiaq">
          Iñupiaq
         </a>
        </li>
        <li>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mapudungun" title="Mapudungun">
          Mapudungun
         </a>
        </li>
        <li>
         <a href="/wiki/Papiamento" title="Papiamento">
          Papiamento
         </a>
        </li>
        <li>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quechua_languages" title="Quechua languages">
          Quechua
         </a>
        </li>
        <li>
         <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
          Spanish
         </a>
        </li>
        <li>
         <a href="/wiki/Tetum_language" title="Tetum language">
          Tetum
         </a>
        </li>
       </ul>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Tone">
        Tone
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tilde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Tone">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_language" title="Vietnamese language">
        Vietnamese
       </a>
       , a tilde over a vowel represents a creaky rising
       <a href="/wiki/Tone_(linguistics)" title="Tone (linguistics)">
        tone
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        ngã
       </i>
       ).
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="International_Phonetic_Alphabet">
        International Phonetic Alphabet
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tilde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: International Phonetic Alphabet">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Phonetics" title="Phonetics">
        phonetics
       </a>
       , a tilde is used as a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IPA_diacritics" title="IPA diacritics">
        diacritic that is placed
       </a>
       above a letter, below it or
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superimpose" title="Superimpose">
        superimposed
       </a>
       onto the middle of it:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        A tilde above a letter indicates
        <a href="/wiki/Nasalization" title="Nasalization">
         nasalization
        </a>
        , e.g.
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ã], [ṽ]
        </span>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        A tilde superimposed onto the middle of a letter indicates
        <a href="/wiki/Velarization" title="Velarization">
         velarization
        </a>
        or
        <a href="/wiki/Pharyngealization" title="Pharyngealization">
         pharyngealization
        </a>
        , e.g.
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ɫ], [z̴]
        </span>
        . If no precomposed
        <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">
         Unicode
        </a>
        character exists, the Unicode character
        <span class="nowrap">
         <span class="monospaced" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">
          U+0334
         </span>
        </span>
        <span style="font-size:125%">
         ◌̴
        </span>
        <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-size: smaller;">
         COMBINING TILDE OVERLAY
        </span>
        can be used to generate one.
       </li>
       <li>
        A tilde below a letter indicates
        <a href="/wiki/Creaky_voice" title="Creaky voice">
         laryngealisation
        </a>
        , e.g.
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [d̰]
        </span>
        . If no precomposed Unicode character exists, the Unicode character
        <span class="nowrap">
         <span class="monospaced" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">
          U+0330
         </span>
        </span>
        <span style="font-size:125%">
         ◌̰
        </span>
        <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-size: smaller;">
         COMBINING TILDE BELOW
        </span>
        can be used to generate one.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Letter_extension">
        Letter extension
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tilde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Letter extension">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Estonian_language" title="Estonian language">
        Estonian
       </a>
       , the symbol ⟨õ⟩ stands for the
       <a href="/wiki/Close-mid_back_unrounded_vowel" title="Close-mid back unrounded vowel">
        close-mid back unrounded vowel
       </a>
       , and it is considered an independent letter.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Other_uses">
        Other uses
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tilde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Other uses">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Some languages and alphabets use the tilde for other purposes:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Arabic_script" title="Arabic script">
         Arabic script
        </a>
        : A symbol resembling the tilde (
        <span class="nowrap">
         <span class="monospaced" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">
          U+0653
         </span>
        </span>
        <span style="font-size:125%">
         ـٓ
        </span>
        <a href="/wiki/Arabic_diacritics#Maddah" title="Arabic diacritics">
         <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-size: smaller;">
          ARABIC MADDAH ABOVE
         </span>
        </a>
        ) is used over the letter ⟨ا⟩ (
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /a/
        </span>
        ) to become
        <big>
         ⟨
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%D8%A2" title="آ">
          آ
         </a>
         ⟩
        </big>
        , denoting a long
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /aː/
        </span>
        sound (
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ʔæː]
        </span>
        ).
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guaran%C3%AD_language" title="Guaraní language">
         Guaraní
        </a>
        : The tilded ⟨
        <a href="/wiki/G%CC%83" title="G̃">
         G̃
        </a>
        ⟩ (note that ⟨G/g⟩ with tilde is not available as a precomposed glyph in
        <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">
         Unicode
        </a>
        ) stands for the
        <a href="/wiki/Velar_nasal" title="Velar nasal">
         velar nasal
        </a>
        consonant. Also, the tilded ⟨y⟩ (⟨Ỹ⟩) stands for the nasalized upper central rounded vowel
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ɨ̃]
        </span>
        . A small number of other alphabets also use ⟨g̃⟩.
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syriac_script" title="Syriac script">
         Syriac script
        </a>
        : A tilde (~) under the letter
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Kaph" title="Kaph">
          Kaph
         </a>
        </i>
        represents a
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [t͡ʃ]
        </span>
        sound, transliterated as
        <i>
         ch
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         č
        </i>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
         <a href="#cite_note-14">
          [14]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Estonian_language" title="Estonian language">
         Estonian
        </a>
        uses tilde inside the letter o to indicate a vowel sound of o unique to that language.
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">
         Unicode
        </a>
        has a
        <a href="/wiki/Combining_character" title="Combining character">
         combining
        </a>
        vertical tilde character:
        <span class="nowrap">
         <span class="monospaced" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">
          U+033E
         </span>
        </span>
        <span style="font-size:125%">
         ◌̾
        </span>
        <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-size: smaller;">
         COMBINING VERTICAL TILDE
        </span>
        . It is used to indicate
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tone_accent" title="Tone accent">
         middle tone
        </a>
        in linguistic transcription of certain dialects of the
        <a href="/wiki/Lithuanian_language" title="Lithuanian language">
         Lithuanian language
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
         <a href="#cite_note-15">
          [15]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Precomposed_Unicode_characters">
        Precomposed Unicode characters

         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Similar_characters">
        Similar characters
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tilde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Similar characters">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       There are many
       <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">
        Unicode
       </a>
       characters for tildes, symbols incorporating tildes, and characters visually similar to a tilde:
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable sortable mw-collapsible Unicode">
       <tr>
        <th class="unsortable">
         Character
        </th>
        <th>
         Code point
        </th>
        <th>
         Name
        </th>
        <th class="unsortable">
         Comments
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         ~
        </td>
        <td>
         U+007E
        </td>
        <td>
         TILDE
        </td>
        <td>
         Same as keyboard tilde. In-line.
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         ˜
        </td>
        <td>
         U+02DC
        </td>
        <td>
         SMALL TILDE
        </td>
        <td>
         Raised but quite small.
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td class="nounderlines">
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CB%B7" title="˷">
          ˷
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         U+02F7
        </td>
        <td>
         MODIFIER LETTER LOW TILDE
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td class="nounderlines">
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CC%83" title="̃">
          ◌̃
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         U+0303
        </td>
        <td>
         COMBINING TILDE
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td class="nounderlines">
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CC%B0" title="̰">
          ◌̰
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         U+0330
        </td>
        <td>
         COMBINING TILDE BELOW
        </td>
        <td>
         Used in
         <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">
          IPA
         </a>
         to indicate
         <a href="/wiki/Creaky_voice" title="Creaky voice">
          creaky voice
         </a>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td class="nounderlines">
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CC%B4" title="̴">
          ◌̴
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         U+0334
        </td>
        <td>
         COMBINING TILDE OVERLAY
        </td>
        <td>
         Used in IPA to indicate
         <a href="/wiki/Velarization" title="Velarization">
          velarization
         </a>
         or
         <a href="/wiki/Pharyngealization" title="Pharyngealization">
          pharyngealization
         </a>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td class="nounderlines">
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CC%BE" title="̾">
          ◌̾
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         U+033E
        </td>
        <td>
         COMBINING VERTICAL TILDE
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td class="nounderlines">
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CD%82" title="͂">
          ◌͂
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         U+0342
        </td>
        <td>
         COMBINING GREEK PERISPOMENI
        </td>
        <td>
         Used as an
         <a href="/wiki/Greek_diacritics#Accents" title="Greek diacritics">
          Ancient Greek
         </a>
         accent under the name "
         <a href="/wiki/Circumflex" title="Circumflex">
          circumflex
         </a>
         "; it can also be written as an
         <a href="/wiki/Inverted_breve" title="Inverted breve">
          inverted breve
         </a>
         .
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td class="nounderlines">
         <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=%CD%8A&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="͊ (page does not exist)">
          ◌͊
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         U+034A
        </td>
        <td>
         COMBINING NOT TILDE ABOVE
        </td>
        <td>
         Raised, small, with slash through.
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td class="nounderlines">

        <td class="nounderlines">

        </td>
        <td>

         </a>
         mark.

       <tr>
        <td class="nounderlines">

        </td>
        <td>

        </td>
        <td>

        <td class="nounderlines">

         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         U+223C
        </td>
        <td>
         TILDE OPERATOR
        </td>
        <td>

        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td class="nounderlines">
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%E2%88%BD" title="∽">
          ∽
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         U+223D
        </td>
        <td>
         REVERSED TILDE
        </td>
        <td>
         In some fonts it is the tilde's simple
         <a href="/wiki/Mirror_image" title="Mirror image">
          mirror image
         </a>
         ; others extend the tips to resemble a
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%E2%88%9E" title="∞">
          ∞
         </a>
         .
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td class="nounderlines">

        <td>

        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td class="nounderlines">
         <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=%E2%B8%AF&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="ⸯ (page does not exist)">
          ⸯ
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         U+2E2F
        </td>
        <td>
         VERTICAL TILDE
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td class="nounderlines">
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%E3%80%9C" title="〜">
          〜
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         U+301C
        </td>
        <td>

        </td>
        <td>
         Used in
         <a href="/wiki/Japanese_punctuation#Wave_dash" title="Japanese punctuation">

         </a>
         .
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td class="nounderlines">

        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
