      </table>
      <h2>
       <span id="ASCII_tilde_.28U.2B007E.29">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="ASCII_tilde_(U+007E)">
        ASCII tilde (U+007E)
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tilde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: ASCII tilde (U+007E)">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <table align="right" border="0" cellpadding="2px" style="margin-left:2em">
       <tr>
        <td align="right">
         Serif:
        </td>
        <td style="font-size:large; font-family:serif">
         —~—
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td align="right">
         Sans-serif:
        </td>
        <td style="font-size:large; font-family:sans-serif">
         —~—
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td align="right">
         Monospace:
        </td>
        <td style="font-size:large; font-family:monospace">
         —~—
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td colspan="2" style="font-size:small">
         A tilde between two em dashes
         <br/>
         in three font families
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:162px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dot_printer_ASCII0x7C%2B.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="288" data-file-width="640" height="72" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Dot_printer_ASCII0x7C%2B.png/160px-Dot_printer_ASCII0x7C%2B.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Dot_printer_ASCII0x7C%2B.png/240px-Dot_printer_ASCII0x7C%2B.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Dot_printer_ASCII0x7C%2B.png/320px-Dot_printer_ASCII0x7C%2B.png 2x" width="160"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dot_printer_ASCII0x7C%2B.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Raised tilde from a
         <a href="/wiki/Dot_matrix_printer" title="Dot matrix printer">
          dot matrix printer
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Most modern
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proportional_font" title="Proportional font">
        proportional fonts
       </a>
       align plain
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spacing_character" title="Spacing character">
        spacing
       </a>
       tilde at the same level as
       <a href="/wiki/Dash" title="Dash">
        dashes
       </a>
       , or only slightly upper. This distinguishes it from a
       <i>
        small tilde
       </i>
       ( ˜ ), which is always raised. But in some
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monospace_font" title="Monospace font">
        monospace fonts
       </a>
       , especially used in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Text_user_interface" title="Text user interface">
        text user interfaces
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/ASCII" title="ASCII">
        ASCII
       </a>
       tilde character is raised too. This apparently is a legacy of
       <a href="/wiki/Typewriter" title="Typewriter">
        typewriters
       </a>
       , where pairs of similar spacing and combining characters relied on one glyph. Even in
       <a href="/wiki/Line_printer" title="Line printer">
        line printers
       </a>
       ' age character repertoires were often not large enough to distinguish between plain tilde, small tilde and combining tilde. Overprinting of a letter by the tilde was a working method of
       <a href="/wiki/Combining_character" title="Combining character">
        combining a letter
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Punctuation">
        Punctuation
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tilde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Punctuation">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The tilde (~) is used in various ways in punctuation:
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Range">
        Range
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tilde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Range">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In some languages (though not generally in English)
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       , a tilde-like wavy dash may be used as
       <a href="/wiki/Punctuation" title="Punctuation">
        punctuation
       </a>
       (instead of an unspaced
       <a href="/wiki/Hyphen" title="Hyphen">
        hyphen
       </a>
       or
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/En-dash" title="En-dash">
        en-dash
       </a>
       ) between two
       <a href="/wiki/Number" title="Number">
        numbers
       </a>
       , to indicate a
       <a href="/wiki/Interval_(mathematics)" title="Interval (mathematics)">
        range
       </a>
       rather than
       <a href="/wiki/Subtraction" title="Subtraction">
        subtraction
       </a>
       or a hyphenated number (such as a part number or model number). For example, "12~15" means "12 to 15", "~3" means "up to three", and "100~" means "100 and greater".
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_(language)" title="Japanese (language)">
        Japanese
       </a>
       and other
       <a href="/wiki/East_Asian_languages" title="East Asian languages">
        East Asian languages
       </a>
       almost always use this convention, but it is often done for clarity in some other languages as well.
       <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">
        Chinese
       </a>
       uses the wavy dash and full-width em dash interchangeably for this purpose. In English, the tilde is often used to express ranges and model numbers in
       <a href="/wiki/Electronics" title="Electronics">
        electronics
       </a>
       , but rarely in formal grammar or in type-set documents, as a wavy dash preceding a number sometimes represents an approximation (see below).
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Approximation">
        Approximation
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tilde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Approximation">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Before a number the tilde can mean "approximately"; "~42" means "approximately 42".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Japanese">
        Japanese
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tilde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Japanese">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Further information:
       <a href="/wiki/Japanese_punctuation#Wave_dash" title="Japanese punctuation">
        Japanese punctuation § Wave dash
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <b>
        wave dash
       </b>
       <span style="font-weight: normal">
        (
        <span lang="ja" xml:lang="ja">
         波ダッシュ
        </span>
        <span style="display:none">
         ,
        </span>
        <i>
         nami dasshu
        </i>
        )
       </span>
       is used for various purposes in Japanese, including to denote ranges of numbers, in place of dashes or brackets, and to indicate origin. The wave dash is also used to separate a title and a subtitle in the same line, as a
       <a href="/wiki/Colon_(punctuation)" title="Colon (punctuation)">
        colon
       </a>
       is used in English.
      </p>
      <p>
       When used in conversations via email or instant messenger it may be used as a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sarcasm_mark" title="Sarcasm mark">
        sarcasm mark
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The sign is used as a replacement for the
       <a href="/wiki/Ch%C5%8Donpu" title="Chōonpu">
        chouon
       </a>
       , katakana character, in Japanese, extending the final syllable.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Unicode_and_Shift_JIS_encoding_of_wave_dash">
        Unicode and Shift JIS encoding of wave dash
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tilde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Unicode and Shift JIS encoding of wave dash">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:208px;max-width:208px">
        <div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:102px;max-width:102px">
         <div class="thumbimage">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wave_Dash.svg">
           <img alt="Correct JIS wave dash." data-file-height="427" data-file-width="427" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Wave_Dash.svg/100px-Wave_Dash.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Wave_Dash.svg/150px-Wave_Dash.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Wave_Dash.svg/200px-Wave_Dash.svg.png 2x" width="100"/>
          </a>
         </div>
         <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
          Correct JIS wave dash.
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:102px;max-width:102px">
         <div class="thumbimage">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wave_Dash2.svg">
           <img alt="Previous Unicode wave dash (incorrect)." data-file-height="427" data-file-width="427" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Wave_Dash2.svg/100px-Wave_Dash2.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Wave_Dash2.svg/150px-Wave_Dash2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Wave_Dash2.svg/200px-Wave_Dash2.svg.png 2x" width="100"/>
          </a>
         </div>
         <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
          Previous Unicode wave dash (incorrect).
         </div>
        </div>
        <div style="clear:left">
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In practice the
       <b>
        full-width tilde
       </b>
       <span style="font-weight: normal">
        (
        <span lang="ja" xml:lang="ja">
         全角チルダ
        </span>
        <span style="display:none">
         ,
        </span>
        <i>
         zenkaku chiruda
        </i>
        )
       </span>
       , Unicode U+FF5E, is often used instead of the
       <b>
        wave dash
       </b>
       <span style="font-weight: normal">
        (
        <span lang="ja" xml:lang="ja">
         波ダッシュ
        </span>
        <span style="display:none">
         ,
        </span>
        <i>
         nami dasshu
        </i>
        )
       </span>
       , Unicode U+301C, because the
       <a href="/wiki/Shift_JIS" title="Shift JIS">
        Shift JIS
       </a>
       code for the wave dash, 0x8160, which is supposed to be mapped to U+301C,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       is instead mapped to U+FF5E
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Code_page_932_(Microsoft_Windows)" title="Code page 932 (Microsoft Windows)">
        Windows code page 932
       </a>
       (Microsoft's
       <a href="/wiki/Code_page" title="Code page">
        code page
       </a>
       for Japanese), a widely used extension of Shift JIS.
      </p>
      <p>
       This avoided a shape definition error in the Unicode code charts: the wave dash reference glyph in JIS / Shift JIS
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jisplane1_20-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-jisplane1-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       matches the Unicode reference glyph for
       <b>
        U+FF5E
       </b>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       while the reference glyph for
       <b>
        U+301C
       </b>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       was reflected, incorrectly,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-errata8_23-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-errata8-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       when Unicode imported the JIS wave dash. In other platforms such as the
       <a href="/wiki/Classic_Mac_OS" title="Classic Mac OS">
        classic Mac OS
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/MacOS" title="MacOS">
        macOS
       </a>
       , 0x8160 is correctly mapped to U+301C. It is generally difficult, if not impossible, for users of Japanese Windows to type U+301C, especially in legacy, non-Unicode applications.
      </p>
      <p>
       The current Unicode reference glyph for U+301C has been corrected
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-errata8_23-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-errata8-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       to match the JIS standard
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       in response to a 2014 proposal, which noted that while the existing Unicode reference glyph had been matched by fonts from the discontinued
       <a href="/wiki/Windows_XP" title="Windows XP">
        Windows XP
       </a>
       , all other major platforms including later versions of Microsoft Windows matched the JIS reference glyph for U+301C.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The JIS / Shift JIS wave dash is still formally mapped to U+301C as of
       <a href="/wiki/JIS_X_0213" title="JIS X 0213">
        JIS X 0213
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       whereas the
       <a href="/wiki/WHATWG" title="WHATWG">
        WHATWG
       </a>
       Encoding Standard used by
       <a href="/wiki/HTML5" title="HTML5">
        HTML5
       </a>
       follows Microsoft in mapping 0x8160 to U+FF5E.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These two code points have a similar or identical glyph in several fonts, reducing the confusion and incompatibility.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Mathematics">
        Mathematics
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tilde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Mathematics">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="As_a_unary_operator">
        As a unary operator
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tilde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: As a unary operator">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       A tilde in front of a single quantity can mean "approximately", "about" or "of the same
       <a href="/wiki/Order_of_magnitude" title="Order of magnitude">
        order of magnitude
       </a>
       as."
      </p>
      <p>
       In written
       <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_logic" title="Mathematical logic">
        mathematical logic
       </a>
       , the tilde represents
       <a href="/wiki/Negation" title="Negation">
        negation
       </a>
       : "~
       <i>
        p
       </i>
       " means "not
       <i>
        p
       </i>
       ", where "
       <i>
        p
       </i>
       " is a
       <a href="/wiki/Proposition" title="Proposition">
        proposition
       </a>
       . Modern use often replaces the tilde with the negation symbol (¬) for this purpose, to avoid confusion with
       <a href="/wiki/Equivalence_relation" title="Equivalence relation">
        equivalence relations
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="As_a_relational_operator">
        As a relational operator
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tilde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: As a relational operator">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">
        mathematics
       </a>
       , the tilde operator (Unicode U+223C), sometimes called "twiddle", is often used to denote an
       <a href="/wiki/Equivalence_relation" title="Equivalence relation">
        equivalence relation
       </a>
       between two objects. Thus "
       <span class="texhtml">
        <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">
         x
        </span>
        ~
        <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">
         y
        </span>
       </span>
       " means "
       <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">
        x
       </span>
       is
       <a href="/wiki/Equivalence_relation" title="Equivalence relation">
        equivalent
       </a>
       to
       <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">
        y
       </span>
       ". It is a weaker statement than stating that
       <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">
        x
       </span>
       <a href="/wiki/Equality_(mathematics)" title="Equality (mathematics)">
        equals
       </a>
       <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">
        y
       </span>
       . The expression "
       <span class="texhtml">
        <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">
         x
        </span>
        ~
        <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">
         y
        </span>
       </span>
       " is sometimes read aloud as "
       <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">
        x
       </span>
       twiddles
       <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">
        y
       </span>
       ", perhaps as an analogue to the verbal expression of "
       <span class="texhtml">
        <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">
         x
        </span>
        =
        <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">
         y
        </span>
       </span>
       ".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The tilde can indicate approximate equality in a variety of ways. It can be used to denote the
       <a href="/wiki/Asymptotic_analysis" title="Asymptotic analysis">
        asymptotic equality
       </a>
       of two functions. For example,
       <span class="texhtml">
        <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">
         f
        </span>
        (
        <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">
         x
        </span>
        ) ~
        <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">
         g
        </span>
        (
        <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">
         x
        </span>
        )
       </span>
       means that
       <span class="texhtml">
        lim
        <sub>
         <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">
          x
         </span>
         → ∞
        </sub>
        <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">
         f
        </span>
        (
        <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">
         x
        </span>
        ) ∕
        <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">
         g
        </span>
        (
        <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">
         x
        </span>
        ) = 1
       </span>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wolfram_tilde_4-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-wolfram_tilde-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       A tilde is also used to indicate "
       <a href="/wiki/Approximation" title="Approximation">
        approximately
       </a>
       equal to" (e.g. 1.902 ~= 2). This usage probably developed as a typed alternative to the
       <a href="/wiki/File:Libra.svg" title="File:Libra.svg">
        libra symbol
       </a>
       used for the same purpose in written mathematics, which is an equal sign with the upper bar replaced by a bar with an upward hump, bump, ︎or loop in the middle (︍︍♎︎) or, sometimes, a tilde (≃). The symbol "≈" is also used for this purpose.︎
      </p>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">
        physics
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">
        astronomy
       </a>
       , a tilde can be used between two expressions (e.g.
       <span class="texhtml">
        <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">
         h
        </span>
        ~ 10
        <sup>
         −34
        </sup>
        J s
       </span>
       ) to state that the two are of the same
       <a href="/wiki/Order_of_magnitude" title="Order of magnitude">
        order of magnitude
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wolfram_tilde_4-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-wolfram_tilde-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Statistics" title="Statistics">
        statistics
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Probability_theory" title="Probability theory">
        probability theory
       </a>
       , the tilde means "is distributed as";
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wolfram_tilde_4-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-wolfram_tilde-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       see
       <a href="/wiki/Random_variable" title="Random variable">
        random variable
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       A tilde can also be used to represent geometric
       <a href="/wiki/Similarity_(geometry)" title="Similarity (geometry)">
        similarity
       </a>
       (e.g.
       <span class="texhtml">
        ∆
        <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">
         ABC
        </span>
        ~ ∆
        <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">
         DEF
        </span>
       </span>
       , meaning
       <a href="/wiki/Triangle" title="Triangle">
        triangle
       </a>
       <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">
        ABC
       </span>
       is similar to
       <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">
        DEF
       </span>
       ). A triple tilde (
       <b>
        ≋
       </b>
       ) is often used to show
       <a href="/wiki/Congruence_(geometry)" title="Congruence (geometry)">
        congruence
       </a>
       , an equivalence relation in geometry.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="As_an_accent">
        As an accent
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tilde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: As an accent">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The symbol "
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle {\tilde {f}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mover>
                <mi>
                 f
                </mi>
                <mo stretchy="false">
                 ~
                 <!-- ~ -->
                </mo>
               </mover>
              </mrow>
             </mrow>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle {\tilde {f}}}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="{\tilde {f}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f6cb99679a4b79cb5ca3c242811bd91220c91f2e" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:1.699ex; height:3.009ex;"/>
       </span>
       " is often pronounced "eff twiddle" or, particularly in American English, "eff wiggle".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This can be used to denote the
       <a href="/wiki/Fourier_transform" title="Fourier transform">
        Fourier transform
       </a>
       of
       <i>
        f
       </i>
       , or a
       <a href="/wiki/Lift_(mathematics)" title="Lift (mathematics)">
        lift
       </a>
       of
       <i>
        f
       </i>
       , and can have a variety of other meanings depending on the context.
      </p>
      <p>
       A tilde placed below a letter in mathematics can represent a
       <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_vector" title="Euclidean vector">
        vector
       </a>
       quantity (e.g.
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle (x_{1},x_{2},x_{3},\ldots ,x_{n})={\underset {^{\sim }}{\mathbf {x} }}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mo stretchy="false">
              (
             </mo>
             <msub>
              <mi>
               x
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mn>
                1
               </mn>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <mo>
              ,
             </mo>
             <msub>
              <mi>
               x
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mn>
                2
               </mn>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <mo>
              ,
             </mo>
             <msub>
              <mi>
               x
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mn>
                3
               </mn>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <mo>
              ,
             </mo>
             <mo>
              …
              <!-- … -->
             </mo>
             <mo>
              ,
             </mo>
             <msub>
              <mi>
               x
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi>
                n
               </mi>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <mo stretchy="false">
              )
             </mo>
             <mo>
              =
             </mo>
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <munder>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi mathvariant="bold">
                 x
                </mi>
               </mrow>
               <msup>
                <mi>
                </mi>
                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                 <mo>
                  ∼
                  <!-- ∼ -->
                 </mo>
                </mrow>
               </msup>
              </munder>
             </mrow>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle (x_{1},x_{2},x_{3},\ldots ,x_{n})={\underset {^{\sim }}{\mathbf {x} }}}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="(x_{1},x_{2},x_{3},\ldots ,x_{n})={\underset {^{\sim }}{\mathbf {x} }}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/5de2ffb491fa88d2974445d3c1ce4368e03d2bc7" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:23.265ex; height:4.009ex;"/>
       </span>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Statistics" title="Statistics">
        statistics
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Probability_theory" title="Probability theory">
        probability theory
       </a>
       , a tilde placed on top of a variable is sometimes used to represent the
       <a href="/wiki/Median" title="Median">
        median
       </a>
       of that variable; thus
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle {\tilde {\mathbf {y} }}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mover>
                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                 <mi mathvariant="bold">
                  y
                 </mi>
                </mrow>
                <mo stretchy="false">
                 ~
                 <!-- ~ -->
                </mo>
               </mover>
              </mrow>
             </mrow>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle {\tilde {\mathbf {y} }}}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="{\tilde {\mathbf {y} }}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/23775aafab5b3b95bf0bdca2fa06e6362b516b72" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:1.411ex; height:2.509ex;"/>
       </span>
       would indicate the median of the variable
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbf {y} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mi mathvariant="bold">
               y
              </mi>
             </mrow>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle \mathbf {y} }
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="\mathbf {y} " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/bb25a040b592282dc2a254c3117e792c3c81161f" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:1.411ex; height:2.009ex;"/>
       </span>
       . A tilde over the letter n (
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle {\tilde {n}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mover>
                <mi>
                 n
                </mi>
                <mo stretchy="false">
                 ~
                 <!-- ~ -->
                </mo>
               </mover>
              </mrow>
             </mrow>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle {\tilde {n}}}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="{\tilde {n}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/632ce72740d78d163f59e5812dab93f1d22ff2c7" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.395ex; height:2.176ex;"/>
       </span>
       ) is sometimes used to indicate the
       <a href="/wiki/Harmonic_mean" title="Harmonic mean">
        harmonic mean
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Physics">
        Physics
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tilde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Physics">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Often in physics, one can consider an equilibrium solution to an equation, and then a perturbation to that equilibrium. For the variables in the original equation (for instance
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle X}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mi>
              X
             </mi>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle X}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="X" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/68baa052181f707c662844a465bfeeb135e82bab" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.98ex; height:2.176ex;"/>
       </span>
       ) a substitution
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle X\to x+{\tilde {x}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mi>
              X
             </mi>
             <mo stretchy="false">
              →
              <!-- → -->
             </mo>
             <mi>
              x
             </mi>
             <mo>
              +
             </mo>
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mover>
                <mi>
                 x
                </mi>
                <mo stretchy="false">
                 ~
                 <!-- ~ -->
                </mo>
               </mover>
              </mrow>
             </mrow>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle X\to x+{\tilde {x}}}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="X\to x+{\tilde {x}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c0eefb9461cc8b6279633b4b685f9fd539fc78ec" style="vertical-align: -0.505ex; width:11.094ex; height:2.343ex;"/>
       </span>
       can be made, where
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle x}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mi>
              x
             </mi>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle x}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="x" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/87f9e315fd7e2ba406057a97300593c4802b53e4" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.33ex; height:1.676ex;"/>
       </span>
       is the equilibrium part and
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle {\tilde {x}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mover>
                <mi>
                 x
                </mi>
                <mo stretchy="false">
                 ~
                 <!-- ~ -->
                </mo>
               </mover>
              </mrow>
             </mrow>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle {\tilde {x}}}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="{\tilde {x}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4f5c5435030c952a58a756e691ea64f60c1bd240" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.33ex; height:2.176ex;"/>
       </span>
       is the perturbed part.
      </p>
      <p>
       A tilde is also used in
       <a href="/wiki/Particle_physics" title="Particle physics">
        particle physics
       </a>
       to denote the hypothetical
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supersymmetric" title="Supersymmetric">
        supersymmetric
       </a>
       partner. For example, an
       <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">
        electron
       </a>
       is referred to by the letter
       <i>
        e
       </i>
       , and its
       <a href="/wiki/Superpartner" title="Superpartner">
        superpartner
       </a>
       the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Selectron_(particle)" title="Selectron (particle)">
        selectron
       </a>
       is written
       <i>
        ẽ
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Economics">
        Economics
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tilde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Economics">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       For relations involving preference,
       <a href="/wiki/Economist" title="Economist">
        economists
       </a>
       sometimes use the tilde to represent
       <a href="/wiki/Preference_(economics)#Notation" title="Preference (economics)">
        indifference
       </a>
       between two or more bundles of goods. For example, to say that a consumer is indifferent between bundles
       <i>
        x
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        y
       </i>
       , an economist would write
       <i>
        x
       </i>
       ~
       <i>
        y
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Electronics">
        Electronics
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tilde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Electronics">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       It can approximate the sine wave symbol (∿,
       <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">
        U+
       </a>
       223F), which is used in
       <a href="/wiki/Electronics" title="Electronics">
        electronics
       </a>
       to indicate
       <a href="/wiki/Alternating_current" title="Alternating current">
        alternating current
       </a>
       , in place of +, −, or ⎓ for
       <a href="/wiki/Direct_current" title="Direct current">
        direct current
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Computing">
        Computing
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tilde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Computing">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Directories_and_URLs">
        Directories and URLs
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tilde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Directories and URLs">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       On
       <a href="/wiki/Unix" title="Unix">
        Unix
       </a>
       -like
       <a href="/wiki/Operating_system" title="Operating system">
        operating systems
       </a>
       (including
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AIX" title="AIX">
        AIX
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BSD" title="BSD">
        BSD
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Linux" title="Linux">
        Linux
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mac_OS_X" title="Mac OS X">
        Mac OS X
       </a>
       ), tilde normally indicates the current user's
       <a href="/wiki/Home_directory" title="Home directory">
        home directory
       </a>
       . For example, if the current user's home directory is
       <span class="monospaced" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">
        /home/bloggsj
       </span>
       , then the command
       <span class="monospaced" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">
        cd ~
       </span>
       is equivalent to
       <span class="monospaced" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">
        cd /home/bloggsj
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="monospaced" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">
        cd $HOME
       </span>
       , or
       <span class="monospaced" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">
        cd
       </span>
       . This convention derives from the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lear-Siegler" title="Lear-Siegler">
        Lear-Siegler
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/ADM-3A" title="ADM-3A">
        ADM-3A
       </a>
       terminal in common use during the 1970s, which happened to have the tilde symbol and the word "Home" (for moving the cursor to the upper left) on the same key.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2008)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       When prepended to a particular username, the tilde indicates that user's home directory (e.g.,
       <span class="monospaced" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">
        ~janedoe
       </span>
       for the home directory of user
       <span class="monospaced" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">
        janedoe
       </span>
       , such as
       <span class="monospaced" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">
        /home/janedoe
       </span>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Used in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uniform_Resource_Locator" title="Uniform Resource Locator">
        URLs
       </a>
       on the
       <a href="/wiki/World_Wide_Web" title="World Wide Web">
        World Wide Web
       </a>
       , it often denotes a personal website on a
       <a href="/wiki/Unix" title="Unix">
        Unix
       </a>
       -based server. For example,
       <span class="monospaced" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">
        http://www.example.com/~johndoe/
       </span>
       might be the personal web site of John Doe. This mimics the Unix shell usage of the tilde. However, when accessed from the web, file access is usually directed to a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subdirectory" title="Subdirectory">
        subdirectory
       </a>
       in the user's home directory, such as
       <span class="monospaced" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">
        /home/
        <i>
         username
        </i>
        /public_html
       </span>
       or
       <span class="monospaced" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">
        /home/
        <i>
         username
        </i>
        /www
       </span>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In URLs, the characters
       <span class="monospaced" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">
        <a href="/wiki/Percent-encoding" title="Percent-encoding">
         ~
        </a>
       </span>
       (or
       <span class="monospaced" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">
        %7e
       </span>
       ) may substitute for tilde if an input device lacks a tilde key.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Thus,
       <span class="monospaced" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">
        http://www.example.com/~johndoe/
       </span>
       and
       <span class="monospaced" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">
        http://www.example.com/%7Ejohndoe/
       </span>
       will behave in the same manner.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Computer_languages">
        Computer languages
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tilde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Computer languages">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The tilde is used in the
       <a href="/wiki/AWK" title="AWK">
        AWK
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Programming_language" title="Programming language">
        programming language
       </a>
       as part of the pattern match operators for
       <a href="/wiki/Regular_expression" title="Regular expression">
        regular expressions
       </a>
       :
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <code>
         <i>
          variable
         </i>
         ~ /
         <i>
          regex
         </i>
         /
        </code>
        returns true if the variable is matched.
       </li>
       <li>
        <code>
         <i>
          variable
         </i>
         !~ /
         <i>
          regex
         </i>
         /
        </code>
        returns false if the variable is matched.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       A variant of this, with the plain tilde replaced with
       <code>
        =~
       </code>
       , was adopted in
       <a href="/wiki/Perl" title="Perl">
        Perl
       </a>
       , and this semi-standardization has led to the use of these operators in other programming languages, such as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ruby_programming_language" title="Ruby programming language">
        Ruby
       </a>
       or the
       <a href="/wiki/SQL" title="SQL">
        SQL
       </a>
       variant of the database
       <a href="/wiki/PostgreSQL" title="PostgreSQL">
        PostgreSQL
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/APL_(programming_language)" title="APL (programming language)">
        APL
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/MATLAB" title="MATLAB">
        MATLAB
       </a>
       , tilde represents the monadic logical function NOT.
      </p>
      <p>
       In the
       <a href="/wiki/C_(programming_language)" title="C (programming language)">
        C
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/C%2B%2B" title="C++">
        C++
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/C_Sharp_(programming_language)" title="C Sharp (programming language)">
        C#
       </a>
       programming languages, the tilde character is used as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bitwise_NOT" title="Bitwise NOT">
        bitwise NOT
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Operators_in_C_and_C%2B%2B" title="Operators in C and C++">
        operator
       </a>
       , following the notation in logic (an
       <code>
        !
       </code>
       causes a logical NOT, instead). In C++ and C#, the tilde is also used as the first character in a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Class_(computer_science)" title="Class (computer science)">
        class
       </a>
       's
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Method_(computer_science)" title="Method (computer science)">
        method
       </a>
       name (where the rest of the name must be the same name as the class) to indicate a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Destructor_(computer_science)" title="Destructor (computer science)">
        destructor
       </a>
       – a special method which is called at the end of the
       <a href="/wiki/Object_lifetime" title="Object lifetime">
        object's life
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In ASP.NET application tilde ('~') is used as a shortcut to the root of the application's virtual directory.
      </p>
      <p>
       In the
       <a href="/wiki/Cascading_Style_Sheets" title="Cascading Style Sheets">
        CSS
       </a>
       stylesheet language, the tilde is used for the indirect adjacent combinator as part of a selector.
      </p>
      <p>
       In the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/D_programming_language" title="D programming language">
        D programming language
       </a>
       , the tilde is used as an
       <a href="/wiki/Array_data_structure" title="Array data structure">
        array
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Concatenation" title="Concatenation">
        concatenation
       </a>
       operator, as well as to indicate an object destructor and bitwise not operator. Tilde operator can be overloaded for user types, and binary tilde operator is mostly used to merging two objects, or adding some objects to set of objects. It was introduced because plus operator can have different meaning in many situations. For example, what to do with "120" + "14" ? Is this a string "134" (addition of two numbers), or "12014" (concatenation of strings) or something else? D disallows + operator for arrays (and strings), and provides separate operator for concatenation (similarly
       <a href="/wiki/PHP" title="PHP">
        PHP
       </a>
       programming language solved this problem by using dot operator for concatenation, and + for number addition, which will also work on strings containing numbers).
      </p>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Eiffel_(programming_language)" title="Eiffel (programming language)">
        Eiffel
       </a>
       , the tilde is used for object comparison. If
       <i>
        a
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        b
       </i>
       denote objects, the boolean expression
       <i>
        a
       </i>
       ~
       <i>
        b
       </i>
       has value true if and only if these objects are equal, as defined by the applicable version of the library routine
       <i>
        is_equal
       </i>
       , which by default denotes field-by-field object equality but can be redefined in any class to support a specific notion of equality. If
       <i>
        a
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        b
       </i>
       are references, the object equality expression
       <i>
        a
       </i>
       ~
       <i>
        b
       </i>
       is to be contrasted with
       <i>
        a
       </i>
       =
       <i>
        b
       </i>
       which denotes reference equality. Unlike the call
       <i>
        a
       </i>
       .
       <i>
        is_equal
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        b
       </i>
       ), the expression
       <i>
        a
       </i>
       ~
       <i>
        b
       </i>
       is
       <a href="/wiki/Type_safety" title="Type safety">
        type-safe
       </a>
       even in the presence of
       <a href="/wiki/Covariance_and_contravariance_(computer_science)" title="Covariance and contravariance (computer science)">
        covariance
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In the
       <a href="/wiki/Groovy_(programming_language)" title="Groovy (programming language)">
        Apache Groovy programming language
       </a>
       the tilde character is used as an operator mapped to the bitwiseNegate() method.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Given a String the method will produce a java.util.regex.Pattern. Given an integer it will negate the integer bitwise like in different C variants.
       <code>
        =~
       </code>
       and
       <code>
        ==~
       </code>
       can in Groovy be used to match a regular expression.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Haskell_(programming_language)" title="Haskell (programming language)">
        Haskell
       </a>
       , the tilde is used in type constraints to indicate type equality.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Also, in pattern-matching, the tilde is used to indicate a lazy pattern match.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In the
       <a href="/wiki/Inform" title="Inform">
        Inform
       </a>
       programming language, the tilde is used to indicate a quotation mark inside a quoted string.
      </p>
      <p>
       In "text mode" of the
       <a href="/wiki/LaTeX" title="LaTeX">
        LaTeX
       </a>
       typesetting language a tilde diacritic can be obtained using, e.g.,
       <code>
        \~{n}
       </code>
       , yielding "ñ". A stand-alone tilde can be obtained by using
       <code>
        \textasciitilde
       </code>
       or
       <code>
        \string~
       </code>
       . In "math mode" a tilde diacritic can be written as, e.g.,
       <code>
        \tilde{x}
       </code>
       . For a wider tilde
       <code>
        \widetilde
       </code>
       can be used. The
       <code>
        \sim
       </code>
       command produce a tilde-like binary relation symbol that is often used in mathematical expressions, and the double-tilde
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%E2%89%88" title="≈">
        ≈
       </a>
       is obtained with
       <code>
        \approx
       </code>
       . The
       <code>
        url
       </code>
       package also supports entering tildes directly, e.g.,
       <code>
        \url{http://server/~name}
       </code>
       . In both text and math mode, a tilde on its own (
       <code>
        ~
       </code>
       ) renders a white space with no line breaking.
      </p>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/MediaWiki" title="MediaWiki">
        MediaWiki
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Syntax_(programming_languages)" title="Syntax (programming languages)">
        syntax
       </a>
       , four tildes are used as a shortcut for a user's signature.
      </p>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Common_Lisp" title="Common Lisp">
        Common Lisp
       </a>
       , the tilde is used as the prefix for format specifiers in format strings.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Max/MSP" title="Max/MSP">
        Max/MSP
       </a>
       , a tilde is used to denote objects that process at the computer's sampling rate, i.e. mainly those that deal with sound.
      </p>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Standard_ML" title="Standard ML">
        Standard ML
       </a>
       , the tilde is used as the prefix for negative numbers and as the unary negation operator.
      </p>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/OCaml" title="OCaml">
        OCaml
       </a>
       , the tilde is used to specify the label for a labeled parameter.
      </p>
      <p>
       In Microsoft's SQL Server
       <a href="/wiki/Transact-SQL" title="Transact-SQL">
        Transact-SQL (T-SQL)
       </a>
       language, the tilde is a unary
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bitwise_NOT#NOT" title="Bitwise NOT">
        Bitwise NOT
       </a>
       operator.
      </p>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/JavaScript" title="JavaScript">
        JavaScript
       </a>
       , the tilde is used as a unary
       <a href="/wiki/Bitwise_operation#NOT" title="Bitwise operation">
        bitwise complement
       </a>
       (or bitwise negation) operation (
       <code>
        ~number
       </code>
       ). Because JavaScript internally uses floats and the bitwise complement only works on integers, numbers are stripped of their decimal part before applying the operation. This has also given rise to using two tildes
       <code>
        ~~number
       </code>
       as a short syntax for a cast to integer (numbers are stripped of their decimal part and changed into their complement, and then back. The net result is thus only the removal of the decimal part). For positive numbers, this is equivalent to the mathematical
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Floor_and_ceiling_functions" title="Floor and ceiling functions">
         floor
        </a>
       </i>
       function.
      </p>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Object_REXX" title="Object REXX">
        Object REXX
       </a>
       , the twiddle is used as a "message send" symbol. For example,
       <code>
        Employee.name~lower()
       </code>
       would cause the
       <code>
        lower()
       </code>
       method to act on the object
       <code>
        Employee
       </code>
       's
       <code>
        name
       </code>
       attribute, returning the result of the operation.
       <code>
        ~~
       </code>
       returns the object that received the method rather than the result produced. Thus it can be used when the result need not be returned or when cascading methods are to be used.
       <code>
        team~~insert("Jane")~~insert("Joe")~~insert("Steve")
       </code>
       would send multiple concurrent
       <code>
        insert
       </code>
       messages, thus invoking the
       <code>
        insert
       </code>
       method three consecutive times on the
       <code>
        team
       </code>
       object.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Backup_filenames">
        Backup filenames
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tilde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Backup filenames">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The dominant
       <a href="/wiki/Unix" title="Unix">
        Unix
       </a>
       convention for naming backup copies of files is appending a tilde to the original file name. It originated with the
       <a href="/wiki/Emacs" title="Emacs">
        Emacs
       </a>
       text editor
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2009)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       and was adopted by many other editors and some command-line tools.
      </p>
      <p>
       Emacs also introduced an elaborate numbered backup scheme, with files named
       <span class="monospaced" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">
        filename.~1~
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="monospaced" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">
        filename.~2~
       </span>
       and so on. It didn't catch on, probably because
       <a href="/wiki/Version_control" title="Version control">
        version control
       </a>
       software does this better.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2009)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Microsoft_filenames">
        Microsoft filenames
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tilde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Microsoft filenames">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The tilde was part of
       <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">
        Microsoft
       </a>
       's
       <a href="/wiki/Filename_mangling" title="Filename mangling">
        filename mangling
       </a>
       scheme when it extended the
       <a href="/wiki/File_Allocation_Table" title="File Allocation Table">
        FAT
       </a>
       file system standard to support long filenames for
       <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Windows" title="Microsoft Windows">
        Microsoft Windows
       </a>
       . Programs written prior to this development could only access filenames in the so-called
       <a href="/wiki/8.3_filename" title="8.3 filename">
        8.3 format
       </a>
       —the filenames consisted of a maximum of eight characters from a restricted character set (e.g. no spaces), followed by a period, followed by three more characters. In order to permit these legacy programs to access files in the FAT file system, each file had to be given two names—one long, more descriptive one, and one that conformed to the 8.3 format. This was accomplished with a name-mangling scheme in which the first six characters of the filename are followed by a tilde and a digit. For example, "
       <span class="monospaced" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">
        Program Files
       </span>
       " might become "
       <span class="monospaced" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">
        PROGRA~1
       </span>
       ".
      </p>
      <p>
       The tilde symbol is also often used to prefix hidden temporary files that are created when a document is opened in Windows. For example, when a document "Document1.doc" is opened in Word, a file called "~$cument1.doc" is created in the same directory. This file contains information about which user has the file open, to prevent multiple users from attempting to change a document at the same time.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Other_uses_2">
        Other uses
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tilde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Other uses">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computer_programmers" title="Computer programmers">
        Computer programmers
       </a>
       use the tilde in various ways and sometimes call the symbol (as opposed to the diacritic) a
       <b>
        squiggle
       </b>
       ,
       <b>
        squiggly
       </b>
       , or
       <b>
        twiddle
       </b>
       . According to the
       <a href="/wiki/Jargon_File" title="Jargon File">
        Jargon File
       </a>
       , other synonyms sometimes used in programming include
       <b>
        not
       </b>
       ,
       <b>
        approx
       </b>
       ,
       <b>
        wiggle
       </b>
       ,
       <b>
        enyay
       </b>
       (after
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/%C3%91" title="Ñ">
         eñe
        </a>
       </i>
       ) and (humorously)
       <b>
        sqiggle
       </b>
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">
          /
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">
            ˈ
           </span>
           <span title="'s' in 'sigh'">
            s
           </span>
           <span title="'k' in 'kind'">
            k
           </span>
           <span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">
            ɪ
           </span>
           <span title="/ɡ/: 'g' in 'guy'">
            ɡ
           </span>
           <span title="/əl/: 'le' in 'bottle'">
            əl
           </span>
          </span>
          /
         </a>
        </span>
       </span>
       . It is used in many languages as a binary inversion operator, swapping a number's binary 1's and 0's for example ~10 (binary ~1010) is equal to 5 (binary 0101).
      </p>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Perl_6" title="Perl 6">
        Perl 6
       </a>
       , "~~" is used instead of "
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%3D~" title="=~">
        =~
       </a>
       ".
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Juggling_notation">
        Juggling notation
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tilde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Juggling notation">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       In the
       <a href="/wiki/Juggling_notation" title="Juggling notation">
        juggling notation
       </a>
       system Beatmap, tilde can be added to either "hand" in a pair of fields to say "cross the arms with this hand on top".
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mills_Mess" title="Mills Mess">
        Mills Mess
       </a>
       is thus represented as (~2x,1)(1,2x)(2x,~1)*.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
        <a href="#cite_note-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Keyboards">
        Keyboards
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tilde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Keyboards">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Where a tilde is on the keyboard depends on the computer's language settings according to the following chart. On many keyboards it is primarily available through a
       <a href="/wiki/Dead_key" title="Dead key">
        dead key
       </a>
       that makes it possible to produce a variety of
       <a href="/wiki/Precomposed_character" title="Precomposed character">
        precomposed characters
       </a>
       with the diacritic.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       In that case, a single tilde can typically be inserted with the dead key followed by the space bar, or alternatively by striking the dead key twice in a row.
      </p>
      <p>
       To insert a tilde with the dead key, it is often necessary to simultaneously hold down the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alt_Gr" title="Alt Gr">
        Alt Gr
       </a>
       key. On the keyboard layouts that include an
       <i>
        Alt Gr
       </i>
       key, it typically takes the place of the right-hand
       <a href="/wiki/Alt_key" title="Alt key">
        Alt key
       </a>
       . With a
       <a href="/wiki/Macintosh" title="Macintosh">
        Macintosh
       </a>
       either of the Alt/
       <a href="/wiki/Option_key" title="Option key">
        Option
       </a>
       keys function similarly.
      </p>
      <p>
       In the US and European
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Windows" title="Windows">
        Windows
       </a>
       systems, the
       <a href="/wiki/Alt_code" title="Alt code">
        Alt code
       </a>
       for a single tilde is
       <code>
        126
       </code>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       For Mac use option+'n' key
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th>
         Keyboard
        </th>
        <th>
         Insert a single tilde (~)
        </th>
        <th>
         Insert a precomposed character with tilde (e.g. ã)
        </th>
       </tr>

          </kbd>

        <td>
         The dead key is not generally used for inserting characters with tilde; when followed
         <p>
          by
          <code>
           [ a c e l n o s x z ]
          </code>
          , it results in
          <code>
           [ ą ć ę ł ń ó ś ź ż ]
          </code>
          instead.
         </p>
        </td>
